<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Gastrodia elata genome provides insights into plant adaptation to heterotrophy"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2018 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-04-24"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="We present the 1.06&#8201;Gb sequenced genome of Gastrodia elata, an obligate mycoheterotrophic plant, which contains 18,969 protein-coding genes. Many genes conserved in other plant species have been deleted from the G. elata genome, including most of those for photosynthesis. Additional evidence of the influence of genome plasticity in the adaptation of this mycoheterotrophic lifestyle is evident in the large number of gene families that are expanded in G. elata, including glycoside hydrolases and urease that likely facilitate the digestion of hyphae are expanded, as are genes associated with strigolactone signaling, and ATPases that may contribute to the atypical energy metabolism. We also find that the plastid genome of G. elata is markedly smaller than that of green plant species while its mitochondrial genome is one of the largest observed to date. Our report establishes a foundation for studying adaptation to a mycoheterotrophic lifestyle. Gastrodia elata is an obligate mycoheterotrophic plant with highly reduced leaves and bracts in scape. Here, Yuan et al sequence and analyze its 1.06 Gb genome which provides insights in adaptation to a lifestyle of heterotrophy."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>We present the 1.06âGb sequenced genome of <i>Gastrodia elata</i>, an obligate mycoheterotrophic plant, which contains 18,969 protein-coding genes. Many genes conserved in other plant species have been deleted from the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome, including most of those for photosynthesis. Additional evidence of the influence of genome plasticity in the adaptation of this mycoheterotrophic lifestyle is evident in the large number of gene families that are expanded in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, including glycoside hydrolases and urease that likely facilitate the digestion of hyphae are expanded, as are genes associated with strigolactone signaling, and ATPases that may contribute to the atypical energy metabolism. We also find that the plastid genome of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> is markedly smaller than that of green plant species while its mitochondrial genome is one of the largest observed to date. Our report establishes a foundation for studying adaptation to a mycoheterotrophic lifestyle.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Symbiotic associations between plants and fungi (mycorrhizae) began about 450 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Heckman, D. S. et al. Molecular evidence for the early colonization of land by fungi and plants. Science 293, 1129â1133 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d24589e730">1</a></sup>. Most mycorrhizal associations are mutualistic, such that the host plant and mycorrhizal fungi exchange nutrients with each other<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="And, S. E. S. &amp; Gianinazzi-Pearson, V. Physiological interactions between symbionts in vesicular-arbuscular mycorrhizal plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 39, 221â244 (1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24589e734">2</a></sup>. However, mycoheterotrophs have evolved a special type of plantâfungi symbiosis in which a plant gets fixed carbon and other nutrients from fungal partners, rather than from photosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hynson, N. A., Preiss, K., Gebauer, G. &amp; Bruns, T. D. Isotopic evidence of full and partial myco-heterotrophy in the plant tribe Pyroleae (Ericaceae). New Phytol. 182, 719 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24589e738">3</a></sup>. One of the most interesting characteristics of orchids is the reliance on fungi for seed germination and nutrient absorption, for example, through formation of mycorrhiza with fungi. Over 99% of orchids show partial mycoheterotrophy in which young plants obtain carbon (C) nutrients from fungi prior to the development of green leaves, while adult plants are autotrophic. The extreme type of mycoheterotrophy in orchids is obligate mycoheterotrophy, in which plants are achlorophyllous (lack chlorophyll) throughout their life cycle and therefore fully dependent on fungi for nutrition.</p><p><i>Gastrodia elata</i> (Orchidaceae) is an orchid popularly used in traditional Chinese medicine that has a fully mycoheterotrophic lifestyle with highly reduced leaves and bracts in scape, although field guides and systematists often refer to the plants as leafless<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Leake &amp; Jonathan, R. Plants parasitic on fungi: unearthing the fungi in myco-heterotrophs and debunking the âsaprophyticâ plant myth. Mycologist 19, 113â122 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d24589e747">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tsai, C. C. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis of Gastrodia elata (Orchidaceae) in response to fungus symbiosis to identify gastrodin biosynthesis-related genes. BMC Genom. 17, 1â16 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d24589e750">5</a></sup>. During its life cycle, in associates with at least two types of fungi: <i>Mycena</i> for seed germination and <i>Armillaria mellea</i> for plant growth. To obtain nutrition, it forms an association with <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zhang, W. J. &amp; Li, B. F. The biological relationship of Gastrodia elata and Armillaria mellea. Bull. Bot. 22, 57â62 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d24589e766">6</a></sup> and more than 80% of its ~36-month lifespan is spent underground as a tuber (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1a</a>). These features of the plant are putative adaptions to its obligate mycoheterotrophic lifestyle. <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> thus offers the possibility of obtaining a valuable insight into the genetic basis of mycoheterotrophy. Here we present a high-quality reference genome assembly of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (Orchidaceae), and use it to investigate the molecular basis of its full mycoheterotrophic life cycle. The observations presented here will be of value for functional ecological studies seeking to understand the mechanisms and evolutionary basis of plantâfungal associations.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-03423-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_3423_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-03423-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_3423_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="746" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>Gastrodia elata</i> life cycle and gene-family contraction. <b>a</b> The main developmental stages of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. Seeds develop into a protocorm stage without requiring <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> (A). The protocorm then differentiates into a corm stage after commencing its association with <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i>; note that lateral buds develop into juvenile tubers (B). Young and immature tubers (C). Mature tuber with an emergent young scape (D). Scape (stem and inflorescence) of mature plants (E). <b>b</b> Phylogenetic tree of 14 plant species including <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. The red dot represents a calibration point determined from the timetree website. <b>c</b> Bar graph of the number of protein-coding genes in each of the species. Single-copy orthologs include common orthologs with one copy in specific species. Multi-copy orthologs include common orthologs with multiple copy numbers in specific species. Other orthologs include genes from families shared in 2â13 species. âEudicotâ clusters with eudicots. âMonocotsâ clusters with monocotyledonous plants. âOrichdâ clusters with <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>. âUnclusteredâ include genes that cannot be clustered into gene families. <b>d</b> Analysis of gene numbers in the genomes of four species for the nucleotide-binding site gene family (NBS), the pathogenesis-related protein (PR) family (Gel <i>G</i>. <i>elata,</i> Peq <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, Dof <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>, Aco <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i>). Numbers in circles represent the number of family members in each genome, and numbers with plus or minus signs indicate, respectively, the number of duplicated or deleted genes. <b>e</b> The plastid genomes of <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> (outer circle) and <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (inner circle). Red, protein; orange, rRNA; green, tRNA; gray, genes lost from the plastid genome of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and annotation</h3><p>The genome of a <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> individual was sequenced using a whole-genome shotgun (WGS) approach (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">1</a>). Through K-mer distribution analysis, the genome size was estimated to be 1.18âGb (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">1</a>). The assembly consisted of 3779 scaffolds, with a scaffold N50 of 4.9âMb (total lengthâ=â1061.09âMb) and contig N50 of 68.9âkb (total lengthâ=â1025.5âMb) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">2</a>). Overall, 98.51% of the raw sequence reads could be mapped to the assembly, suggesting that our assembly results contained comprehensive genomic information (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">3</a>). Gene region completeness was evaluated by RNA-Seq data (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">4</a>): of the 80,646 transcripts assembled by Trinity, 98.66% could be mapped to our genome assembly, and 94.41% were considered as complete (more than 90% of the transcript could be aligned to one continuous scaffold). The completeness of gene regions was further assessed using CEGMA (conserved core eukaryotic gene mapping approach): 239 of 248 (96.37%) conserved core eukaryotic genes from CEGMA were captured in our assembly, and 217 (87.5%) of these were complete (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>Much of the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome (66.18%) was occupied by transposable elements (TEs). Class I (retrotransposons) and Class II (DNA transposons) TEs accounted for 55.94% and 4.38% of the genome, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">6</a>). Long terminal repeats (LTRs) formed the most abundant category of TE, with LTR/Gypsy and LTR/Copia occupying 45.04% and 7.10% of the genome, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">7</a>). Global activity of LTRs was similar between <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> and <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>, while <i>Dendrobium officinale</i> presented a recent burst of LTR activities (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">2a</a>). Compared to <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, all LTR families in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> had fewer members, except a substantive expansion of del family (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">2b</a>). Through a combination of ab initio prediction, homology search, and RNA sequence-aided prediction, 18,969 protein-coding genes were predicted in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome. Of these genes, 81.6% were functionally annotated (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">9</a>) and 88.69% had detectable transcripts in an RNA-seq analysis of protocorms, tubers (juvenile, immature, and mature tubers), and scapes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">10</a>). Our transcriptomics analysis revealed that there were 10,548 differentially expressed genes among the five growth stages; these differentially expressed genes clustered into five distinct groups that were representative of the particular stages of growth of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">11</a> and Supplementary Note).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Phylogeny and whole-genome duplication</h3><p>Comparison of the sequenced genomes of the orchid species <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1040">7</a></sup>, and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Yan, L. et al. The genome of Dendrobium officinale illuminates the biology of the important traditional Chinese orchid Herb. Mol. Plant 8, 922â934 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1050">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, G. Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Report. 6, 19029 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1053">9</a></sup> indicated that they diverged approximately 67 million years ago (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">4</a>). Two ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) events are evident in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome; these events can also be discerned in the genomes of <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> suggesting they occurred prior to the divergence of the three orchid species (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">5</a>). The older WGD event might represent the heWGD event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1085">10</a></sup> shared by most monocots, while the younger WGD event were likely shared by all extant orchids and might contribute to the divergence of orchid, as suggested in <i>Apostasia shenzhenica</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhang, G. Q. et al. The Apostasia genome and the evolution of orchids. Nature 549, 379â383 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1093">11</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Extensive gene lost in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome</h3><p>Compared to <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> (29,431 protein-coding genes) and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> (28,910 protein-coding genes), <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> has a relative small proteome size (18,969 protein-coding genes). The estimated proteome size of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> is the smallest theoretical proteome so far identified among angiosperm genomes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">12</a>). Comparison of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> genes that have functional annotation information revealed global gene set reduction in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome. For example, almost all second level gene ontology (GO) categories had fewer genes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> than in the other two species, and 9 of these categories (16.7%) were significantly reduced (Fisherâs Exact test, <i>p</i>â&lt;â0.05, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">13</a>). We also found that several Pfam domain families were significantly reduced in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">14</a>). Among the 14 angiosperm used in the phylogenetic analysis, <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> had the lowest number of gene families; moreover, <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> had on average the lowest number of genes in each gene family (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1c</a>, and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">15</a>). This consistently low number of genes and gene families suggests that many gene families have been eliminated from the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome, and further suggests that many of the remaining gene families have contracted. Gene family expansion and contraction analysis based on maximum likelihood modeling of gene gain and loss confirmed that 3586 gene families had undergone contraction in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, much more compared to the other two orchid genomes (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">16</a>). A Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) analysis, which assessed 956 orthologous groups with genes present as single-copy in at least 90% of plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1229">12</a></sup>, revealed that 195 (20.4%) highly-conserved genes were missing from the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome. This rate of absence is much higher than in the genomes of the 13 land species that were included in this analysis (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">17</a>). All of these analyses indicate that <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> has undergone extensive gene losses, even for genes that were conserved in other plant species that have also undergone extensive lost events.</p><p>The absence of these genes is unlikely to be due to genome assembly problems because 98.66% of the transcripts assembled from transcriptome data could be mapped to the assembly. Another possibility is that several genes were missed due to gene prediction problems. By mapping RNA reads onto the annotated genome, we found that the majority of RNA reads (&gt;86%) from all <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tissues could be mapped to annotated exon regions (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">18</a>). This rate of mapping was comparable to that achieved in the well-annotated rice genome and higher than in the <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">18</a>). Through analysis of gene synteny among <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> and <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>, we detected 2961 gene deletion events in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> versus <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, and 3120 gene deletion events in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> versus <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">19</a>). Further TBLASTN searches of these deleted genes recovered less than 3% of them. Of these genes, fewer than 15% were supported by RNA-seq data (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">19</a>). Both the RNA mapping results and the synteny deletion analysis confirmed that our gene prediction was comprehensive; thus, the possibility of missing gene annotations was low. Finally, PCR amplification of 18 lost genes (<i>atp</i>D, <i>atp</i>G, <i>Ihc</i>A, <i>Ihc</i>B, <i>psa</i>D, <i>psa</i>F, <i>psa</i>L, <i>psa</i>N, <i>psb</i>O, <i>psb</i>R, <i>psb</i>Y, <i>psb</i>27, <i>psb</i>28, <i>pet</i>C, <i>pet</i>E, <i>ICS</i>, <i>DHAR</i>, and <i>TRX</i>) confirmed that all were absent from the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">20</a>). Thus, the global gene losses in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> represent evolutionary events, and might be the result of adaption to an obligate mycoheterotrophic lifestyle.</p><p>Both pseudogenizations and genome rearrangements contributed to the gene lost process of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. We found 876 and 1080 pseudogenes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> using <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> genes as seeds, respectively (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">21</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">24</a>). Through a whole-genome alignment between <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> and <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, we found 487 genes were lost due to local rearrangements (SV genes, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">25</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">26</a>). Functional genes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> were located closer to transposable elements than to pseudogenes and SV genes, suggesting that transposable element did not play significant role during the gene lost processes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">10</a>). Thus the gene lost processes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> might be dominated by random mutations as we found many pseudogenes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. Notably, compared with the genomes of <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> (pineapple, used in this study as an outgroup) and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (used in this study as an outgroup), the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome has a reduced number of genes related to plant resistance to pathogens, such as the NBS (the nucleotide-binding site) gene family, PR (pathogenesis-related) gene family, and genes of antioxidant proteins (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1d</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">11</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">27</a>). For example, the <i>ICS</i> gene, which is known to function as a primary modulator of salicylic acid-based plant defense responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wildermuth, M. C., Dewdney, J., Wu, G. &amp; Ausubel, F. M. Isochorismate synthase is required to synthesize salicylic acid for plant defence. Nature 414, 562â565 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1523">13</a></sup>, is absent from the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome, illustrating the loss of a key gene for systemic acquired resistance (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">27</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Degeneration of photosynthesis system</h3><p>As <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> does not perform photosynthesis, it was unsurprising that genes enriched for âchloroplastâ and âplastidâ annotations were strongly represented among the missing genes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">28</a>). To further investigate the putative functions of missing genes, we examined genes related to the photosynthetic apparatus, namely Photosystem I, Photosystem II, Cytochrome b<sub>6</sub>f, Cytochrome C<sub>6</sub>, ATP synthase, and Rubisco<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Leister, D. Chloroplast research in the genomic age. Trends Genet. 19, 47â56 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1565">14</a></sup>. Of the 35 nuclear genes coding for photosynthetic apparatus proteins (NEP), only 12 were present in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome; this is significantly fewer than in <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">28</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">29</a>). We assume that these genes were non-functional because their full complements of subunits were not present.</p><p>We also sequenced and assembled the plastid genome of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. We found that the plastid genome of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (35,326âbp) was dramatically restructured and reduced in size, in a similar manner to the reduction in gene number observed for the nuclear genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1e</a>), compared to the plastid genomes of <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> (148,958âbp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Jheng, C.-F. et al. The comparative chloroplast genomic analysis of photosynthetic orchids and developing DNA markers to distinguish Phalaenopsis orchids. Plant Sci. 190, 62â73 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1632">15</a></sup> and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> (152,221âbp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Yang, P. et al. The complete chloroplast genome sequence of Dendrobium officinale. Mitochondrial DNA 27, 1262â1264 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1642">16</a></sup>, the two other orchid species with sequenced genomes. The plastid genomes of these two species comprise two single-copy regions (a large and a small single-copy region) and the two identical large inverted repeats (IRs) encode 75 and 76 genes, respectively, that are most associated with photosynthesis. The <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> plastid genome has lost one IR and encodes only 19 protein-coding genes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1e</a>), suggesting that <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> is an ancient mycoheterotroph and that its plastid genome is in the last stage of a âdegradation ratchetâ, i.e., retention and loss of the five core nonbioenergetic genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Graham, S. W., Lam, V. K. &amp; Merckx, V. S. Plastomes on the edge: the evolutionary breakdown of mycoheterotroph plastid genomes. New Phytol. 214, 48â55 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1662">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Barrett, C. F. &amp; Davis, J. I. The plastid genome of the mycoheterotrophic Corallorhiza striata (Orchidaceae) is in the relatively early stages of degradation. Am. J. Bot. 99, 1513â1523 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1665">18</a></sup>. Excluding the possibility that these genes were missed by our genome assembly, the transcriptome sequencing analysis indicated that none of the deleted plastid or nuclear encoded genes were expressed in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, while the five core nonbioenergetic genes, <i>trnE</i>, <i>aacD</i>, <i>clpP</i>, <i>ycf1</i>, and <i>ycf2</i>, were moderately to highly expressed in all five stages in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">31</a>). These results clearly show that both the plastid and nuclear genomes of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> have lost most of the genes required for photosynthesis, although the highly degraded plastome is still essential for this full mycoheterotroph.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Expansion of mitochondrial genome</h3><p>Although the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome has clearly undergone extensive gene loss, we found that 430 gene families (19 by a significant margin), containing 1532 genes (184 by a significant margin), showed expansion in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> compared to <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>, and <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">33</a>). These genes are enriched for GO terms related to several metabolic processes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">32</a>). We speculate that these expanded genes are related in some way to the functional requirements of the obligate mycoheterotrophic lifestyle of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. We first sequenced and assembled the mitochondrial genome to explore this idea, and the mitochondrial genome <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> is markedly expanded in size (1339âkb, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig2">2b</a>) compared to the mitochondrial genomes of most other seed plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Skippington, E., Barkman, T. J., Rice, D. W. &amp; Palmer, J. D. Miniaturized mitogenome of the parasitic plant Viscum scurruloideum is extremely divergent and dynamic and has lost all nad genes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, E3515âE3524 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1783">19</a></sup>. Thirty-seven protein-coding genes were annotated, and one subunit of mitochondrial ATP synthase, atp4, had two copies in the mitochondrial genome of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> and was highly expressed in the cortex layer (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">34</a>). In addition, 36 of the genes had detectable expression in mature tubers (epidermis, cortex, and parenchymal cell) using a tissue-specific qPCR-based analysis (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">34</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-03423-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_3423_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-03423-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_3423_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="778" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Gene expansion in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> and microbial community analysis. <b>a</b> REViGO semantic similarity scatter plot of Biology Process Gene Ontology terms for expanded genes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. In semantic spaces, the proximity between circles represents relatedness (similarity) of the GO terms. Similar GO terms are close together in the plot. The axes in the plot have no intrinsic meaning, but were used to measure pairwise similarities between GO terms. Color indicates degree of enrichment for each process presented as the <i>p</i>-value from the hyper-geometric test. <b>b</b> The draft mitochondrial genome of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. Nineteen contigs are manually displayed as a circle, including 12 circular contigs in orange (ornamented with stars) and 7 linear contigs (in blue). The genes are indicated in the middle circle, and are color coded as follows: <i>trn</i> (blue), <i>rrn</i> (light blue), <i>atp</i> (red), and other protein-coding genes (black). The duplicated <i>atp</i> genes and their fragments are detailed in the inner circle. The duplicated genes are suffixed with âbâ, and the gene fragments are suffixed with âfragmentâ. <b>c</b> Gene expression heat map of the normalized RNA-Seq data for genes encoding the monocot mannose-binding lectin antifungal proteins (GAFP) in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Yang, P. et al. The complete chloroplast genome sequence of Dendrobium officinale. Mitochondrial DNA 27, 1262â1264 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1859">16</a></sup>. The units indicate the expression levels of different gene members of <i>GAFP</i> in the protocorm, juvenile tuber, immature tuber, mature tuber, and scape of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (only shown where the gene expression level RPKMâ&gt;â1, <i>n</i>â=â3). <b>d</b> Venn diagrams showing the number of shared and unique fungal and bacterial operational taxonomic units (OTUs) based on the ITS and 16S sequence analyses in protocorms, juvenile tubers, immature tubers, and mature tubers of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. OTUs showed the composition and abundance of the microbe species, which were defined at 3% dissimilarity</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Management of symbiotic microbials</h3><p>We next explored how gene expansion in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> may have contributed to its association and interactions with fungal microbiota. The monocot mannose-binding lectin antifungal protein family (GAFP) of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> contains 20 genes, compared to only 3 in <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> and 0 in <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">27</a>). GAFP proteins have been documented to inhibit the growth of both ascomycete and basidiomycete fungal plant pathogens in vitro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Cox, K. D., Layne, D. R., Scorza, R. &amp; Schnabel, G. Gastrodia anti-fungal protein from the orchid Gastrodia elata confers disease resistance to root pathogens in transgenic tobacco. Planta 224, 1373â1383 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1930">20</a></sup>. More than 80% of the <i>GAFP</i> genes were highly expressed in protocorms and juvenile tubers, the growth stages that occur before <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> establishes a stable symbiotic association with <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig2">2c</a>). 4-Hydroxybenzyl alcohol (<i>p</i>-PA), the precursor of the phytoalexin gastrodin is a major phenolic compound of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Bai, Y. et al. De novo biosynthesis of Gastrodin in Escherichia coli. Metab. Eng. 35, 138 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1962">21</a></sup>. The expression of <i>p</i>-PA biosynthesis genes (e.g., cinnamate 4-hydroxylase, <i>C4H</i>, alcohol dehydrogenase, <i>ADH</i>, hydroxybenzaldehyde synthase, <i>HBS</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Bai, Y. et al. De novo biosynthesis of Gastrodin in Escherichia coli. Metab. Eng. 35, 138 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24589e1979">21</a></sup> was relatively high in protocorms and juvenile tubers. Ultra performance liquid chromatography coupled with quadrupole time-of-flight mass spectrometry (UPLC-ESI-Q-TOF-MS) metabolite analysis revealed that <i>p</i>-HA is present in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tubers but not in <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> hyphae (sampled from tuber, wood, and PDA medium). <i>S</i>-(<i>p</i>-HA)-glutathione was detected in both <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tubers and in <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> hyphae sampled from tubers (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">12</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">35</a>), which putatively suggests that <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> may transport this phytoalexin to <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> and prevent the excessive growth of <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i>. To investigate the effect of <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> on microbial management in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, we performed a 16S ribosomal (rRNA) and rDNA ITS sequencing analysis and found that the diversity of bacterial and microbial species was significantly lower during the protocorm stage than at other growth stages (<i>p</i>â&lt;â0.05), which was consistent with the pattern of gene expression of <i>GAFP</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig2">2c</a>, d, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">37</a>). This increased diversity of bacteria and fungi during the juvenile tuber to mature tuber periods implies that a compatible mycorrhizal fungus (<i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i>) can affect the structure of the microbial community associated with its host and greatly reduce the antifungal and antibacterial activities as a symbiotic association with <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> is established.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Signaling and nutrition transfer in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>
                        </h3><p>Without the ability to perform photosynthesis, <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> depends completely on its symbiotic fungus for nutrition. It is thus obvious that the signaling pathways related to the establishment of this symbiotic relationship are crucial for <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. Some of the mechanisms underlying the symbiotic interaction between <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> are similar to those for interactions between other plants and arbuscular mycorrhizal (AM) fungi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Garcia, K., Delaux, P. M., Cope, K. R. &amp; Ane, J. M. Molecular signals required for the establishment and maintenance of ectomycorrhizal symbioses. New Phytol. 208, 79â87 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2124">22</a></sup>. The <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome contains many of the genes known to participate in AM associations (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">38</a>). Key genes for biosynthesis and secretion of strigolactone were expanded in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (e.g., carotenoid cleavage dioxygenases, CCDs, for biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Delaux, P. M. et al. Origin of strigolactones in the green lineage. New Phytol. 195, 857â871 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2147">23</a></sup> and ABC transporters, PDRs, for secretion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kretzschmar, T. et al. A petunia ABC protein controls strigolactone-dependent symbiotic signalling and branching. Nature 483, 341â344 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2151">24</a></sup>) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">38</a>). It is known that strigolactone can stimulate hyphal branching and development of arbuscular mycorrhizal fungi, which increases the chances of an encounter with a host plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kretzschmar, T. et al. A petunia ABC protein controls strigolactone-dependent symbiotic signalling and branching. Nature 483, 341â344 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2159">24</a></sup>. We conducted growth assays and confirmed that strigolactone had similar branch-inducing effects in <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig3">3b</a>, Supplemental Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">13</a>). The expanded number of genes encoding CCDs and PDRs suggests that <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> has enhanced its ability to interact with <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> to increase the efficiency of the establishment of the symbiotic relationship essential for its nutrition and metabolism. Calmodulin-dependent protein kinase genes of the <i>does-not-make-infections 3</i> subfamily (<i>DMI3</i>) were also doubled or tripled in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (10 genes) compared to <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> (3 genes), <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> (5 genes), and <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> (4 genes); these genes participate in the Ca<sup>2+</sup> spiking process that has been shown to regulate the colonization of plants by fungi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Maillet, F. et al. Fungal lipochitooligosaccharide symbiotic signals in arbuscular mycorrhiza. Nature 469, 58â63 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2222">25</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-03423-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_3423_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-03423-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_3423_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="379" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Strigolactone as a putative signal compound in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> in its mycoheterotrophic symbiotic relationship with <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i>. <b>a</b> Overview of proposed signaling and nutrition transfer in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. The red-labeled genes are expanded in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome and <i>P</i>-value of Fisherâs exact test of gene number &lt;0.05 (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">38</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">40</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">41</a>). ANT, ANT1-like aromatic and neutral amino acid transporters; ArgJ, glutamate N-acetyltransferase; CCD, carotenoid cleavage dioxygenases; COS, chitooligosaccharides; DMI3, does-not-make-infections 3 subfamily; LOS, lipochitooligosaccharides; LysM, LysM-receptor-like kinases; PDR, ABC transporter. P1, <i>p</i>-value of Fisherâs exact test of gene number in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome compared to <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i>, and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>; P2, <i>p</i>-value of Fisherâs exact test of gene number in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome compared to <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>; P3, <i>p</i>-value of Fisherâs exact test of gene number in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome compared to <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i>; P5, <i>p</i>-value of Fisherâs exact test of gene number in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> genome compared to <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>. <b>b</b> Branching of <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> hyphae was significantly promoted after strigolactone treatment (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">13</a>). Scale bar was 5âmm. <b>c</b> Cross sections and micrographs of immature <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tubers in association with <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> (A, epidermis; B, cortex; C, inner parenchyma cells, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">14</a>). The black scale bar on the left was 100âÎ¼m and the white one on the right was 20âÎ¼m</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>After <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> colonizes <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, fungal growth is restricted to its cortex layer (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig3">3c</a>, Supplemental Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">14</a>). We performed a tissue-specific qPCR-based analysis of 10 genes in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tubers and found that PDR transcripts, which mediate secretion of strigolactone to the extracellular space, were highly abundant in the cortex layer (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">15</a>). This finding suggests that <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> may preferentially guide <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> to colonize its cortex layer. Similarly to ATP synthases, we found that some glycoside hydrolases from gene families that have expanded in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome were also highly expressed in the cortex layer, supporting the idea that <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> hyphal walls are digested in the cortex layer of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tubers (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">39</a>). The expanded endo-Î²-1,4-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-xylanase and Î²-glucosidase may have become neofunctionalized to cleave fungal glycan substrates during the digestion of hyphal walls of <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">40</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Fraser, R. N. &amp; Lindberg, B. Polysaccharides elaborated by Armillaria mellea (tricholomataceae): Part I. A water-soluble polysaccharide from the fruit bodies. Carbohydr. Res. 4, 12â19 (1967)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2500">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wargo, P. M. Lysis of the cell wall of Armillaria mellea by enzymes from forest trees. Physiol. Plant Pathol. 5, 99â105 (1975)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2503">27</a></sup>.</p><p>Given that the ANT1-like aromatic and neutral amino acid transporters (ANT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Rentsch, D., Schmidt, S. &amp; Tegeder, M. Transporters for uptake and allocation of organic nitrogen compounds in plants. FEBS Lett. 581, 2281â2289 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2510">28</a></sup> are known to translocate arginine (Arg), which is a key component in nitrogen translocation in arbuscular mycorrhizal fungi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cruz, C. et al. Enzymatic evidence for the key role of arginine in nitrogen translocation by arbuscular mycorrhizal fungi. Plant Physiol. 144, 782â792 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2514">29</a></sup>, it seems likely that Arg in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> is related to mycoheterotrophic symbiosis (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">41</a>). It is known that arginases can hydrolyze Arg into urea in mycelia, which is further hydrolyzed to ammonium and carbonic acid by ureases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Witte, C. P., Rosso, M. G. &amp; Romeis, T. Identification of three urease accessory proteins that are required for urease activation in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 139, 1155â1162 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2531">30</a></sup>. Although <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i>, <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> have only one copy of glutamate N-acetyltransferase (ArgJ), an enzyme of the arginine biosynthesis pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hernandez, V. M. et al. Genetic and biochemical characterization of arginine biosynthesis in Sinorhizobium meliloti 1021. Microbiology 162, 725 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2560">31</a></sup>, <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> has three copies (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">11</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">41</a>). The number of genes encoding ureases is drastically expanded in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (9 genes) compared with <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> (2 genes), <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> (2 genes), <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> (1 gene), and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> (1 gene) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">41</a>). This suggests that urea metabolism might be an important source of nitrogen for <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig3">3a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec99">Conclusion</h3><p>The extensive deletion and expansion of genes, especially the global reduction of gene complements in almost all functional categories in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome, provides a powerful example of how a plant with a fully heterotrophic life cycle has made use of genome plasticity to achieve extensive neo-functionalization and gene loss. Our results establish a unique opportunity for researchers to understand how plants that have abandoned photosynthesis continue to persist and thrive.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant materials and DNA preparation</h3><p>The experimental materials of <i>Gastrodia elata</i> were harvested from Xiaocaoba in Yunnan Province (latitude 27.79Â°N longitude 104.24Â°E) located in the southwestern China. Genome sequencing and assembly was done on the scape of beige-scape <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. Five transcriptomes were sequenced from five different <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tissues (protocorm, juvenile tuber, immature tuber, mature tuber, scape). Four different <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tissues (protocorm, juvenile tuber, immature tuber, mature tuber) were collected to investigate the diversity of microbial communities. High-quality genomic DNA was extracted using the Qiagen DNeasy Plant Mini Kit.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Multiple paired-end and mate-pair libraries were constructed with a spanning size that ranged from 180âbp to 20âkb. Sequencing was conducted on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform. In total, 179.1âGb raw sequencing reads were produced (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">1</a>). Raw sequencing reads were subjected to filtering to remove (1) low quality reads with low quality bases (&gt;50% bases with <i>Q</i>-value â¤8); (2) reads with Ns &gt;10% of the read length; (3) reads with adapter contamination; and (4) duplicated reads caused by PCR during library construction. Filtered data were assembled using ALLpaths-LG (version 44080)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2684">32</a></sup>, where overlapping paired-end reads with an insert size of 230 nucleotides were used as fragment libraries, and all other libraries (&gt;230 nucleotide insert size) were used as jumping libraries. The Allpaths-LG assembly was run with default settings, then a gap filling step was carried out using GapCloser based on the paired-end information of the paired-end reads that had one end mapped to the unique contig and the others located in the gap region (<a href="http://sourceforge.net/projects/soapdenovo2/files/GapCloser">http://sourceforge.net/projects/soapdenovo2/files/GapCloser</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome-quality evaluation</h3><p>To evaluate the completeness of the assembly and the uniformity of the sequencing, all the paired-end reads were mapped to the assembly using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2703">33</a></sup>. The mapping rate was 98.51% and the genome coverage was 99.84%. This result suggested that our assembly results contained almost all the information in the reads (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">3</a>). Gene region completeness was evaluated from the scape tissue, of 80,646 transcripts assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2710">34</a></sup>, 98.66% could be mapped to our genome assembly, and 94.41% were considered as complete (more than 90% of the transcript could be aligned to one continuous scaffold). CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2714">35</a></sup> (Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach) defined a set of conserved protein families that occur in a wide range of eukaryotes, and identified their exonâintron structures in a novel genomic sequence. Through mapping to the 248 core eukaryotic genes, a total of 239 genes with a ratio of 96.37% were found in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">5</a>). Genome completeness was also assessed using BUSCO gene set analysis version 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wildermuth, M. C., Dewdney, J., Wu, G. &amp; Ausubel, F. M. Isochorismate synthase is required to synthesize salicylic acid for plant defence. Nature 414, 562â565 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2728">13</a></sup> which includes a set of 956 single-copy orthologous genes specific to Plantae.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Repetitive elements identification</h3><p>A combined strategy based on homology alignment and de novo search was used to identify repeat elements in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome. For de novo prediction of transposable elements (TEs), we used RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>), RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2753">36</a></sup>, and LTR-Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2757">37</a></sup> with default parameters. For alignment of homologous sequences to identify repeats in the assembled genome, we used RepeatProteinMask and RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) with the rebase library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2769">38</a></sup>. Transposable elements overlapping with the same type of repeats were integrated, while those with low scores were removed if they overlapped more than 80 percent of their lengths and belonged to different types (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Dynamics of long terminal-repeat retrotransposons</h3><p>Intact Long terminal-repeat retrotransposons (LTR) were identified by searching the genomes of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> and <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> with LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2804">39</a></sup> from Genome Tools v1.5.1. The candidate sequences were filtered by two-step procedure to reduce false positives. First, LTRdigest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002â7013 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2808">40</a></sup> was used to identify the primer binding site (PBS) motif based on the predicted tRNA sequences from tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2812">41</a></sup>, and only elements contained PBS were retained; then protein domains (pol, gag and env) in candidate LTR retrotransposons were identified by searching against HMM profiles collected by Gypsy Databas (GyDB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Llorens, C. et al. The gypsy database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D70âD74 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2816">42</a></sup>. Elements contained gag domain, protease domain, reverse transcriptase (RT) domain and integrase domain, which were considered as intact. Second, families of these intact LTR retrotransposons were clustered using the previously described method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="El Baidouri, M. &amp; Panaud, O. Comparative genomic paleontology across plant kingdom reveals the dynamics of TE-driven genome evolution. Genome Biol. Evol. 5, 954â965 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2820">43</a></sup>. Finally, LTRs that did not contain protein domains or that belonged to families with less than 5 members were discarded. The EMBOSS program distmat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European molecular biology open software suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276â277 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2825">44</a></sup> was used to estimate LTR divergence rates between the 5â²- and 3â²- LTR sequences of the intact LTRs (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Gene prediction</h3><p>Gene prediction was conducted through a combination of homology-based prediction, ab initio prediction and transcriptome-based prediction methods. Protein repertoires of plants including <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2845">10</a></sup>, <i>Amborella trichopoda</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Project, A. G. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2851">45</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (phytozomev10), <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (phytozomev10), <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, G. Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Report. 6, 19029 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2867">9</a></sup>, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> (phytozomev10), <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2883">7</a></sup>, <i>Vitus vinifera</i> (phytozomev10), <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> (phytozomev10) and <i>Zea mays</i> (phytozomev10) were downloaded and mapped to the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome using TBLASTN (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1<i>e</i><sup>â5</sup>). The BLAST hits were conjoined by Solar software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Yu, X. J., Zheng, H. K., Wang, J., Wang, W. &amp; Su, B. Detecting lineage-specific adaptive evolution of brain-expressed genes in human using rhesus macaque as outgroup. Genomics 88, 745â751 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2910">46</a></sup>. GeneWise (version 2.4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise.
