Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2915">47</a></sup> was used to predict the exact gene structure of the corresponding genomic region on each BLAST hit. Homology predictions were denoted as âHomology-setâ. RNA-seq data derived from protocorm, juvenile tuber, immature tuber, mature tuber, and scape (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1a</a>) were assembled by Trinity (version 2.0) <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2922">41</a></sup>. The Trinity assembly included 183,515 contigs with an average length of 592âbp. These assembled sequences were aligned against the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome by PASA (Program to Assemble Spliced Alignment)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2932">48</a></sup>. Valid transcript alignments were clustered based on genome mapping location and assembled into gene structures. Gene models created by PASA were denoted as PASA-T-set (PASA Trinity set). Besides, RNA-seq reads were directly mapped to the genome using Tophat (version 2.0.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2937">49</a></sup> to identify putative exon regions and splice junctions; Cufflinks (version 2.1.1) was then used to assemble the mapped reads into gene models (Cufflinks-set). Augustus (version 2.5.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W465âW467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2941">50</a></sup>, GeneID (version)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Parra, G., Blanco, E. &amp; Guigo, R. GeneID in Drosophila. Genome Res. 10, 511â515 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2945">51</a></sup>, GeneScan (version 1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Aggarwal, G. &amp; Ramaswamy, R. Ab initio gene identification: prokaryote genome annotation with GeneScan and GLIMMER. J. Biosci. 27, 7â14 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2949">52</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (version 3.0.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2953">53</a></sup>, and SNAP (version)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2957">54</a></sup> were also used to predict coding regions in the repeat-masked genome. Of these, Augustus, SNAP and GlimmerHMM were trained by PASA-H-set gene models. Gene models generated from all the methods were integrated by EvidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2962">48</a></sup>. Weights for each type of evidence were set as follows: PASA-T-setâ&gt;âHomology-setâ&gt;âCufflinks-setâ&gt;âAugustusâ&gt;âGeneIDâ=âSNAPâ=âGlimmerHMMâ=âGeneScan. The gene models were further updated by PASA2 to generate UTRs, alternative splicing variation information, which generated 26,872 gene models. Gene models only supported by ab initio evidence were filtered out. To reduce the possibility of missing and poorly annotated genes, we invested additional effort in annotating some gene families that could be missed by automated genome annotation, such as NBS-encoding genes. In total, 1943 protein sequences containing an NB-ARC domain were searched against the <i>G. elata</i> genome using TBLASN with a threshold of 1<i>e</i><sup>â5</sup>. All BLAST hits in the genome, together with 5000âbp flanking regions on both sides, were annotated by the GeneWise program. The resulting predictions were surveyed to verify whether they encoded NBS or LRR motifs using Pfam. We also focused on other genes, such as those related to photosynthesis, and transporter, and these were manually annotated through a combination of BLAST search and motif verification. Ultimately, a comprehensive non-redundant reference gene set was produced that contained 18,969 protein-coding gene models. Functional annotation of the protein-coding genes was carried out using BLASTP (<i>E</i>-value cut-off 1<i>e</i>â05) against two integrated protein sequencing databases, SwissProt and TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45â48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2980">55</a></sup>. Protein domains were annotated by searching against InterPro (Version 5.16)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Mulder, N. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterPro and InterProScan: tools for protein sequence classification and comparison. Methods Mol. Biol. 396, 59â70 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2984">56</a></sup> and Pfam (Version 3.0) database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Punta, M. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D290âD301 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2988">57</a></sup>, using InterProScan (version 4.8) and HMMER (version 3.1b1) (<a href="http://hmmer.janelia.org">http://hmmer.janelia.org</a>), respectively. The GO terms for genes were obtained from the corresponding InterPro or Pfam entry. The pathways in which the genes might be involved were assigned by BLAST against the KEGG databases (release 20150831)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d24589e2999">58</a></sup> with the <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 1<i>e</i>â05.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Identification of pseudogenes</h3><p>Pseudogenes in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome were identified by searching against <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> intergenic regions using <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> or <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> protein sequences as the seed sequences (TBLASTN, <i>E</i>-value cut-off 1<i>e</i>â5). Before the BLAST search, regions of the 18,969 true genes were masked. The BLAST hits were conjoined by Solar software. GeneWise was used to predict the pseudogene structures with the â-pseudoâ parameter. Pseudogenes were then classified by PseudoPipe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Zhang, Z. et al. PseudoPipe: an automated pseudogene identification pipeline. Bioinformatics 22, 1437â1439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3049">59</a></sup>. The PseudoPipe program applies a set of sequence identity and completeness cut-off to report a final set of good-quality pseudogene sequences. We used the following cutoffs: amino acid (AA) sequence identity &gt;30% and match length &gt;50 AA to filter out false positives. GeneWise results that fulfilled the cut-off criteria were denoted as high-confidence pseudogenes. High-confidence pseudogenes were then assigned to three categories. (1) Processed/retrotransposed pseudogenes (PSSDs), which formed through retrotransposition. Retrotransposition occurred by reintegration of a cDNA, a reverse transcribed mRNA transcript, into the genome at a new location. (2) Duplicated pseudogenes (DUPs), which formed through gene duplication, following by decay of genes, include frameshifts or premature stop codons. (3) Pseudogenic fragments (FRAGs), which were fragments that have high-sequence similarity to known proteins, but were too decayed to be reliably assessed as processed or duplicated. We used the following criteria to classify PSSDs, DUPs, and FRAGs: (i) PSSDs, exon numberâ=â1, 0.7â&lt;âalign ratioââ¤â0.95, 0.3ââ¤âidentityââ¤â0.95; (ii) DUPs, exon numberâ&gt;â1, 0.3ââ¤âidentityââ¤â0.95, and existing insertion, deletion, termination, or frameshift; (iii) FRAGs, exon numberâ=â1, align ratioâ&lt;â0.7, 0.3ââ¤âidentityââ¤â0.95.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Gene family construction</h3><p>Whole protein-coding gene repertoires from 14 plant genomes including <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i> <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3073">10</a></sup>, <i>A</i>. <i>trichopoda</i> <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Project, A. Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Report. 6, 19029 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3106">9</a></sup>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3115">7</a></sup>, <i>Elaeis oleifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Singh, R. et al. Oil palm genome sequence reveals divergence of interfertile species in old and new worlds. Nature 500, 335â339 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3122">60</a></sup>, <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> (phytozomev10), <i>V</i>. <i>vinifera</i> (phytozomev10), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (JGI), <i>Glycine max</i> (phytozomev10), <i>Picea abies</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3147">61</a></sup>, <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> (ASM242v1) were used to construct a global gene family classification. To remove redundancy caused by alternative splicing variations, we retained only gene models at each gene locus that encoded the longest protein sequence. To exclude putative fragmented genes, genes encoding protein sequences shorter than 50 amino acids were filtered out. All-against-all BLASTp was employed to identity the similarities between filtered protein sequences in these species with an <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 1<i>e</i><sup>â7</sup>. The OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3162">62</a></sup> method was used to cluster genes from these different species into gene families with the parameter of â-inflation 1.5â.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Phylogenetic tree reconstruction</h3><p>Protein sequences from 74 single-copy gene families were used for phylogenetic tree reconstruction. MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3174">63</a></sup> was used to generate multiple sequence alignment for protein sequences in each single-copy family with default parameters. Then, the alignments of each family were concatenated to a super alignment matrix. The super alignment matrix was used for phylogenetic tree reconstruction through maximum likelihood (ML) methods. Before ML reconstruction, we used ProtTest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Abascal, F., Zardoya, R. &amp; Posada, D. ProtTest: selection of best-fit models of protein evolution. Bioinformatics 21, 2104â2105 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3178">64</a></sup> to select the best substitution models. The JTTâ+âIâ+âGâ+âF model was selected as the best-fit model, and RAxMLwas used to reconstruct the phylogenetic tree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688â2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3182">65</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Species divergence time estimation</h3><p>Divergence time between 14 species was estimated using McMctree in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3194">66</a></sup> with the options âcorrelated molecular clockâ and âJC69â model. A Markov Chain Monte Carlo analysis was run for 20,000 generations, using a burn-in of 1000 iterations. Five calibration points were applied in the present study (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#Fig1">1</a>): <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> divergence time (47~52.9 million years ago) <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Xiang, X. et al. Biogeographical diversification of mainland Asian Dendrobium (Orchidaceae) and its implications for the historical dynamics of evergreen broad-leaved forests. J. Biogeogr. 43, 1310â1323 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3214">67</a></sup>, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> and <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i> divergence time (24â84 million years ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Magallon, S., Hilu, K. W. &amp; Quandt, D. Land plant evolutionary timeline: gene effects are secondary to fossil constraints in relaxed clock estimation of age and substitution rates. Am. J. Bot. 100, 556â573 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3231">68</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Magallon, S. Using fossils to break long branches in molecular dating: a comparison of relaxed clocks applied to the origin of angiosperms. Syst. Biol. 59, 384â399 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3234">69</a></sup>, <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> and <i>P</i>. <i>trichocarpa</i> divergence time (65â89 million years ago) <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Wikstrom, N., Savolainen, V. &amp; Chase, M. W. Evolution of the angiosperms: calibrating the family tree. Proc. Biol. Sci. 268, 2211â2220 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3250">70</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Smith, S. USA 107, 5897â5902 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3253">71</a></sup>, <i>P</i>. <i>trichocarpa</i> and <i>G</i>. <i>max</i> divergence time (56â89 million years ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Mulder, N. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterPro and InterProScan: tools for protein sequence classification and comparison. Methods Mol. Biol. 396, 59â70 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3270">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Punta, M. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D290âD301 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3273">57</a></sup>, and, root of land plants (407â557 million years ago) <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Punta, M. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D290âD301 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3277">57</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Gene family expansion and contraction</h3><p>Expansion and contractions of orthologous gene families were determined using CAFÃ 2.2 (Computational Analysis of gene Family Evolution). The program uses a birth and death process to model gene gain and loss over a phylogeny. Large changes in gene family size in a phylogeny were tested by calculating <i>p</i>-values on each branch using the Viterbi method with a randomly generated likelihood distribution. This method calculates exact <i>p</i>-values for transitions between the parent and child family sizes for all branches of the phylogenetic tree. Enrichment of Gene Ontology terms for <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> expanded gene families were summarized and visualized using REVIGO (small list, similarity (0.5), SimRel similarity measure).</p><p>The expanded and contracted families focused on in this study were confirmed using Fisherâs exact test. For each gene family, we compared the gene count of the tested family in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (copy number of the tested family as numerator, total number of genes of the whole genome as denominator) versus the frequency in <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Yan, L. et al. The genome of Dendrobium officinale illuminates the biology of the important traditional Chinese orchid Herb. Mol. Plant 8, 922â934 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3316">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, G. Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Report. 6, 19029 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3319">9</a></sup>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3329">7</a></sup>, <i>A</i>. <i>comosus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3338">10</a></sup>, and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (phytozomev10). In addition, phylogenetic trees were constructed for each family to confirm gene gain or loss events. The extreme case of gene lost was that one gene was absent in the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome. To avoid false positive gene absence events caused by missing gene annotations, a TBLASTN search against the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome was carried out using protein sequences derived from other plant genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">PCR verification of lost genes</h3><p>Total genomic DNAs were extracted by hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) method from <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>, <i>A. comosus</i>, and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i>. PCR was carried out using SpeedSTAR<sup>TM</sup> HS DNA Polymerase (TaKaRa, Japan) and specific gene primers (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">20</a>). PCR products were verified by agarose gel electrophoresis (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Whole-genome duplication analysis</h3><p>A homolog search within the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome was performed using BLASTP (<i>E</i>-valueâ&lt;â1<i>e</i><sup>â7</sup>), and MCscanX was used to identify syntenic blocks within the genome. For each gene pair in a syntenic block, the 4DTv (transversion substitutions at fourfold degenerate sites) distance was calculated, and values of all gene pairs were plotted to identify putative whole-genome duplication events in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Inferring syntenic gene deletions in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>
                        </h3><p>Proteins of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> and <i>D</i>. <i>officinale</i> were aligned using the BLASTp algorithm (<i>E</i>-valueâ&lt;â1<i>e</i><sup>â7</sup>). Alignments with matches of at least 30% identity and coverage higher than 30% were retained for comparison. The best reciprocal BLAST pairs between different genomes were extracted as putative orthologous gene pairs. Then, using gene location information in each species, we identified micro-synteny gene blocks between <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> and the other two orchids. Putative gene loss events were traced from the synteny table using the flanking gene method. Given three genes A, B, and C in order, if gene A and C were presented as collinear orthologs in two genomes, but B was missed in one of the genome (for example, <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>), then gene B was denoted as a possible lost gene in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. To avoid false positives due to the failure of gene annotation, the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> intergenic genomic sequence between A and C was extracted, and a GeneWise prediction in this intergenic region was carried out using the B protein sequence from <i>P</i>. <i>equestris</i> as seed. If the predicted protein could be aligned to the seed protein with coverage &gt;70%, and did not contain frameshift or premature stop codon mutations, this gene loss event was defined as a false positive and filtered out (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Plastid genome</h3><p>Total genomic DNA was extracted using a modified CTAB from silica-dried tissues of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">42</a>). The DNA was sheared to 500âbp, and sequencing libraries were generated using the NEBNext Ultra DNA Library Prep Kit (according to the manufacturerâs protocol) for sequencing on an Illumina Hiseq 2500 at the State Key Laboratory of Systematics and Evolutionary Botany, Chinese Academy of Sciences. The raw reads were filtered using NGSQCTOOLKIT v 2.3.3. The cleaned reads were mapped to <i>Calanthe triplicata</i> in GENEIOUS 9.0 (Biomatters, Inc., Auckland, New Zealand; <a href="http://www.geneious.com">http://www.geneious.com</a>). Used reads were exported and assembled using SOAPDENOVO2. The plastid sequences were extracted from the total contigs using BLASTN 2.2.29+ and the <i>C</i>. <i>triplicata</i> plastome (GenBank ID: NC_024544) as subject sequence. The finished plastome scaffolds were reoriented according to the <i>C</i>. <i>triplicata</i> reference plastome. The boundaries of IRs were determined by BLAST, and finished manually. The plastomes of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> were determined using DOGMA with an <i>e</i>-value of 5, a 60% cut-off for protein-coding genes and 80% cut-off for tRNAs; the GENEIOUS annotation tool was used to determine the plastomes of <i>C</i>. <i>triplicata</i> and <i>Oncidium</i> Gower Ramsey (GenBank ID: NC_014056.1) as references. Linear plastome maps were drawn using OGDRAW.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Mitochondrial genome</h3><p>Mitochondria were isolated from all <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tissues except the rhizome using previously described centrifugation methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Kolodner, R. &amp; Tewari, K. K. Physicochemical characterization of mitochondrial DNA from pea leaves. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 69, 1830â1834 (1972)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3576">72</a></sup>. A modified CTAB method was used to extract mt-DNAs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Skippington, E., Barkman, T. J., Rice, D. W. &amp; Palmer, J. D. Miniaturized mitogenome of the parasitic plant Viscum scurruloideum is extremely divergent and dynamic and has lost all nad genes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, E3515âE3524 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3580">19</a></sup>. The purified mt-DNAs were sequenced on an Illumina Hiseq 2500 to generate 100âbp paired-end reads at the State Key Lab of Systematic and Evolutionary Botany, Chinese Academy of Sciences (Beijing).</p><p>Eleven million raw reads were generated from sequencing and trimmed using Trimmomatic v0.35 to produce low quality reads. All sequenced plant mitochondrial genomes were downloaded from NCBI and used as a local blast database. To minimize the possibility of contaminated reads from plastid or nuclear genomes, filtered reads were first mapped to the local database and mapped reads were subsequently imported into Geneious v10.1.3 (Biomatters, Inc., Auckland, New Zealand; <a href="http://www.geneious.com">http://www.geneious.com</a>) for initial assembly. The contigs generated by the initial assembly were used as seeds for further iterative mapping and extension processes. Velvet and Geneious were alternatively used during assembly with multiple combinations of k-mer lengths.</p><p>In most cases, the extension process of the assembly worked well. In particular, when the head and tail of a contig had an overlapping region and could not be further extended, this contig could be reasonably connected into a circle. Although several circles were produced during assembly, some problems did arise in the extension process. For example, some contigs were displayed as single lines because their boundaries were too difficult to determine due to poly structures or repeats in the mitogenome. The final assembled results were verified by remapping and some ambiguous regions with low coverage were further checked by PCR. Overall, 19 contigs with a total length of 1,340,105âbp were assembled including 12 circles (ranging from 13.5 to 120.6âkb) and 7 single lines (837,015âbp).</p><p>The assembled contigs were firstly annotated by NCBI-BlastN based on the local database with an <i>e</i>-valueâ&lt;â1<i>e</i><sup>â6</sup>. Then, the boundaries of each gene were confirmed by Mitofy and exported as Sequin formatted files. tRNA genes were further predicted by tRNAscan (<a href="http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/">http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/</a>)(Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">43</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec27">Transcriptome library and gene expression analysis</h3><p>The paired-end reads for protocorm, juvenile tuber, immature tuber, mature tuber, scape samples were mapped to the <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> genome using TopHat. The total numbers of aligned reads were normalized by gene length and sequencing depth for an accurate estimation of expression level. We used these normalized read counts (RPKM) as the expression level for each gene. Then, DESeq was used to identify differentially expressed genes (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">44</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">58</a>). A repeated bisection method and a topâdown hierarchical clustering algorithm in gCLUTO (<a href="http://glaros.dtc.umn.edu/gkhome/cluto/gcluto/overview">http://glaros.dtc.umn.edu/gkhome/cluto/gcluto/overview</a>) were used to generate the expression profiles of all differently expressed genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec28">RNA extraction and quantitative PCR</h3><p>Four different <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tissues (epidermis, cortex, parenchymal cell A and B) were collected from three mature tuber samples. Total RNAs were extracted using TRIzolÂ® Reagent (Thermo Fisher Scientific, USA) according to the manufacturerâs instructions. The concentrations and purities of the total RNAs were assessed by a spectrophotometric analysis at 260 and 280ânm. One Î¼g of total RNA was reverse transcribed at 42âÂ°C using TransScriptÂ® Reverse Transcriptase (TransBionovo Co, China) and Oligo(dT)<sub>18</sub> according to the manufacturerâs recommendations. Prior to use in qPCR, cDNA was diluted 1:5 with H<sub>2</sub>O.</p><p>The qPCR reactions were performed in duplicate for each condition using the KAPA SYBRÂ® FAST qPCR Master Mix (KapaBiosystems, USA) and LightCyclerÂ® 480 Real-Time PCR System (Roche, Switzerland). Each reaction consisted of 20âÎ¼L containing 1âÎ¼L of cDNA and 200ânM of each primer (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">59</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#MOESM1">60</a>). The cycling conditions were: denaturation at 95âÂ°C for 3âmin; followed by 45 two-segment cycles of amplification at 95âÂ°C for 10âs, and 60âÂ°C for 30âs in which fluorescence was automatically measured, and one three-segment cycle of 95âÂ°C for 5âs, 65âÂ°C for 1âmin, and 95âÂ°C for 30âs. The baseline adjustment method of the LightCyclerÂ® 480 software was used to determine the Ct in each reaction. Î²-actin was selected as the internal control and the expression levels of tested genes were determined using the comparative Ct (2<sup>âÎÎCt</sup>) method.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec29">Diversity of microbial communities</h3><p>Four different <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> tissues (protocorm, juvenile tuber, immature tuber, mature tuber) were collected and total genomic DNAs were extracted using hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB). The 16S V4 and ITS1 genes in all sample were amplified using the universal primers 515F-806R and ITS5-1737F with a barcode as a marker for distinguishing samples. The PCR was performed with PhusionÂ® High-Fidelity PCR Master Mix (New England Biolabs, Ipswich, MA, UK). PCR products were mixed in equidensity ratios. Then, the mixed PCR products were purified with Qiagen Gel Extraction Kit (Qiagen, Germany). Sequencing libraries were generated using a TruSeqÂ® DNA PCR-Free Sample Preparation Kit (Illumina, USA) following the manufacturerâs recommendations and index codes were added. The library quality was assessed on a Qubit<sup>@</sup> 2.0 Fluorometer (Thermo Scientific) and Agilent Bioanalyzer 2100 system. The library was sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform and 250âbp paired-end reads were generated.</p><p>High-quality sequences were clustered into OTUs defined at 97% similarity. These OTUs were applied for diversity, richness and rarefaction curve analyses using MOTHUR. Taxonomic assignments of OTUs that reached the 97% similarity level were made using the QIIME (quantitative insights into microbial ecology) software package through comparison with the SILVA, Greengene, and RDP databases. Venn diagrams were generated to identify the mutual and specific taxons between groups using R software (<a href="http://www.r-project.org/">http://www.r-project.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec30">Microscopy</h3><p>For identification and analysis of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> infected by <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i>, hand sections were cut through the infection point of an immature tuber, and the sections were then embedded in agar plates. Images were captured using a Zeiss AX10 fluorescence microscope with Ã10 water immersion lenses. Owing to the spontaneous blue fluorescence, both visible and DAPI filters were used to observe the hyphae of <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> with fluorescent microscopy (Zeiss AX10).</p><p>To analyze tissue and cell structures of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> uninfected by <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i>, paraffin sections (10âÎ¼m thickness) were obtained using a Thermo Scientific MicRoM HM 325 sliding microtome. For light microscopic observations of the highly lignified hyphae of <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i>, sections were stained with Fast Green stain reagent to investigate the infected cells of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. After staining, sections were washed by PBS three times, dehydrated through an alcohol (50%, 80%, 90%, 95%, 100%), cleared in xylene, sealed with neutral gum, and observed using a fluorescence microscope (Zeiss AX10).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec31">Quantification of <i>p</i>-HA and <i>S</i>-(<i>p</i>-HA)-glutathione</h3><p>About 0.35âg of frozen fresh <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> were homogenized and ultrasonically extracted for 30âmin in four volumes (gâmL<sup>â1</sup>) water. After centrifugation (13,000ârpm, 10âmin), 100âÎ¼L aliquots of supernatant were mixed with 10âÎ¼l of rutin (101.0âÎ¼gâmL<sup>â1</sup>) for quantification of <i>p</i>-HA (<i>Î»</i> 270ânm, Rt 6.8âmin) and <i>S</i>-(<i>p</i>-HA)-glutathione (<i>m</i>/z 412.12, Rt 8.9âmin) using an UPLC-PDA-ESI-Q-TOF-MSE method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Lai, C. J. et al. Untargeted metabolite analysis-based UHPLC-Q-TOF-MS reveals significant enrichment of p-hydroxybenzyl dimers of citric acids in fresh beige-scape Gastrodia elata (Wutianma). J. Pharm. Biomed. Anal. 140, 287â294 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3799">73</a></sup>. The injection volume was 1âÎ¼L. The contents were determined by the peak intensity ratios of the analyte to rutin (<i>m</i>/z 609.14, 15.5âmin).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec32">Hyphal-branching assay</h3><p>Hyphal branching in <i>A</i>. <i>mellea</i> fungi was evaluated in vitro by the paper disk diffusion method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Akiyama, K., Matsuzaki, K. &amp; Hayashi, H. Plant sesquiterpenes induce hyphal branching in arbuscular mycorrhizal fungi. Nature 435, 824â827 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-03423-5#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d24589e3820">74</a></sup>. Primary hyphae were cultured in PDA medium containing 20âgâL<sup>â1</sup> glucose, 4âgâL<sup>â1</sup> potato powder and 14âgâL<sup>â1</sup> agar. The dishes were cultured in the dark for 5â7 days at 23âÂ°C. Secondary hyphae emerge from primary hyphae and grow upward in a negative geotropic manner in the gel; the growth of secondary hyphae was used for the assay. Test samples were first dissolved in acetone then diluted with 70% ethanol in water. The concentration of test sample solutions of natural 5-deoxy-strigol was adjusted with reference to the calibration of synthetic (Â±)-5-deoxy-strigol in an HPLC analysis. Paper disks (1âcm in length, 8âmm in width) loaded with 15âÎ¼L of test sample solution were placed in front of the tips of the secondary hyphae. The control was on the opposite direction of the paper without 5-deoxy-strigol. Hyphal branch patterns were analyzed at 24âh and 48âh after treatment. The sample was scored as positive for hyphal branching if new hyphal branches formed from the treated secondary hyphae. The assay was repeated at least twice, using between three and five dishes for each concentration.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec33">Data availability</h3><p>Genome data were deposited in GenBank under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/PVEL00000000">PVEL00000000</a> and transcriptome sequence reads were deposited in the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRX2879747">SRX2879747</a>. The standard flowgram format (SFF) files related with bacterial and fungal communities were also deposited in the SRA under study accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRX2875242">SRX2875242</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRX2876148">SRX2876148</a>. Plastid genome data were deposited in GenBank under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/MF163256">MF163256</a>. Mitochondrial genome data were deposited in GenBank under accession numbers <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore">MF070084-MF070102</a></p></div></div></section>

Y.Y. and W.J. coordinated the project and directed work on the reference genome of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. X.J., D.W., and X.M. assembled the plastid and mitochondrial genomes and analyzed gene duplication. X.Z., W.X., M.Z., Z.J., R.L. assembled the reference genome and analyzed genome characterization. J.L. performed experiments of diversity of microbial communities in <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i> during different periods. Y.Y., J.L., X.Z., and L.Z. performed bioinformatic analyzes to annotate the reference genome. J.Z. and C.J. extracted high-quality RNA and qPCR analysis. C.L., T.N., Z.Z., and J.Y. conceived and conducted the analyses of the metabolomics. X.W. and H.P. observed the microscopic characterization of <i>G</i>. <i>elata</i>. J.H., Y.Y., and Y.L. worked on culture of <i>Armillaria mellea</i> and the hyphal-branching assay. D.L., L.W., Y.Y., and Y.Z. collected samples. Y.J. and H.M. verified the gene loss using PCR.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

                        class="js-separator">&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;</span><span>Tao Zhou</span></li></ul>
              
              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-5">
                <i>Frontiers in Plant Science</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
          
        </ul>
      </div>
    </div>
    </section>
